HW4: Greedy and Binary Search

Author

Jasmine Kobayashi

Coding

def greedy_search(list,x):
  # for-loop to iterate through list
  for i in range(len(list)):
    if list[i] == x:
      statement = "Index {index} on list {list_arg} is {element} equal {value}. Found!".format(index = i,list_arg = list,element = list[i],value = x)
      print(statement)
      return i
    else:
      statement = "Index {index} on list {list_arg} is {element} not {value}. Move on!".format(index = i,list_arg = list,element = list[i],value = x)
      print(statement)

  # exiting/finishing loop means that value (x) was not found in list
  return -1
def binary_search(list,x):
  start_ind = 0
  end_ind= len(list) - 1

  while start_ind<=end_ind:
    mid_ind = (start_ind + end_ind)//2
    if list[mid_ind] == x:
      statement = "Index {index} on list {list_arg} is {element} equal {value}. Found!".format(index = mid_ind,list_arg = list,element = list[mid_ind],value = x)
      print(statement)
      return mid_ind
    else:
      if list[mid_ind] < x:
        statement = "Index {index} on list {list_arg} is {element} less than {value}. Move up!".format(index = mid_ind,list_arg = list,element = list[mid_ind],value = x)
        start_ind = mid_ind + 1
      else:
        statement = "Index {index} on list {list_arg} is {element} greater than {value}. Move down!".format(index = mid_ind,list_arg = list,element = list[mid_ind],value = x)
        end_ind = mid_ind - 1
      
      print(statement)

  # exiting/finishing loop means that value (x) was not found in list
  return -1

The slowest run took 18.95 times longer than the fastest. This could mean that an intermediate result is being cached.
1.15 ms  857 s per loop (mean  std. dev. of 7 runs, 100 loops each)

Write your result of running, and a short paragraph to address your findings (is there anything surprising you? why did it happen?)

JK response:

Coding-wise, I believe I got the results I was supposed to. Binary-search found the element in less steps than greedy-search.

And I believe it was intentional to have binary-search not find the value 17 in set2 (since it was not sorted).

I suppose one thing I found surprising was that time complexity does not directly correlate with length of code (number of lines of code). The binary search require more things like nested if-statements, but was found to be more efficient and have a better time complexity (big O). I was wondering about this when we started learning about the different sort methods also.

But I guess the reason this happens, is because the program actually has less things to do when we create conditions so that it doesnt have to iteratively look through and compare every element.

Although, on the other hand, the binary search also has some assumptions embedded within it. The assumption being that the given list is sorted in numerically-ascending order. If the list fails to meet this assumed condition, then theres a chance the search could fail.
